To be one of the few storms to reach this intensity, a storm must have favorable conditions surrounding it, including warm water temperatures, high humidity in the middle part of the atmosphere, and small differences in upper and lower level winds steering the storm.  \n\nThese storms form over tropical oceans, often far from land, so we rely on satellites to measure some properties from which we can estimate their intensity.  Based on these estimates, Haiyan may have had stronger surface winds than in any storm previously observed (195 mph with gusts to 235 mph), but we no longer fly aircraft into storms to directly measure their intensity in the western Pacific, so we have no way to be certain of its exact winds. Regardless of the precise wind speed, the storm was incredibly intense and did immense damage in the Philippines, where it resulted in a tragic number of casualties.\"", "icon": "https://fbstatic-a.akamaihd.net/rsrc.php/v2/yq/r/SC2ZmEkfI-X.png", "type": "link", "id": "30730623774_10151798978013775", "status_type": "shared_story", "description": "\"We had a triple whammy of surge, very high winds and strong rainfall,\" says one climate scientist."}
